        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="280630c0defa43fda67197deef585bd1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7b0f4f01-131c-4e0a-a10b-24dd7e515865'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                     <p>Energy Systems Development Division, Energy Systems Group, Propellants Polymers Chemicals and Materials Entity, Vikram Sarabhai Space Centre, Indian Space Research Organisation, Thiruvananthapuram, Kerala, 695022 India</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Raj+Murugan/Arjun">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Sarojiniamma/Sujatha" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Sujatha Sarojiniamma</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-name">Sujatha Sarojiniamma</p>
                     <p>Energy Systems Development Division, Energy Systems Group, Propellants Polymers Chemicals and Materials Entity, Vikram Sarabhai Space Centre, Indian Space Research Organisation, Thiruvananthapuram, Kerala, 695022 India</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Sarojiniamma/Sujatha">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Sinthai+Appusamy/Ilangovan" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Ilangovan Sinthai Appusamy</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">
                     <p class="author-name">Ilangovan Sinthai Appusamy</p>
                     <p>Energy Systems Development Division, Energy Systems Group, Propellants Polymers Chemicals and Materials Entity, Vikram Sarabhai Space Centre, Indian Space Research Organisation, Thiruvananthapuram, Kerala, 695022 India</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Sinthai+Appusamy/Ilangovan">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d82212606" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium ion batteries are considered as a potential alternative to existing Li-ion batteries. Layered transition metal oxides are found to be suitable cathode candidates for Na-ion batteries. In this study, solid state synthesis of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with different Mg substitutions is reported. The synthesized materials are characterized thoroughly using X-ray diffraction (XRD), field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM), energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS), inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES), and x-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) analysis. SEM analysis reveals the morphology of synthesized materials to layered structure with a particle size in the range of 5–50&nbsp;µm. Powder XRD shows all synthesized materials of P2 structure irrespective of Mg substitution. A spurious P3 phase is observed in 0% and 5% of Mg substituted samples which are absent in 10% and 15% of Mg substituted materials. Electrochemical performance analysis shows specific capacity reduces with Mg substitution whereas mid-voltage reaches maximum up to 3.5&nbsp;V with 10% substitution. 10% Mg substituted NMO shows a specific capacity 105 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage ranges of 2–4.3&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> with excellent cycling stability and rate capability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202200519-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The renewed interest in the electrification of transport vehicles and large-scale energy storage from renewable energy is gaining huge momentum among the scientific and industrial communities in order to overcome critical issues such as climate change, global warming, and ever-depleting fossil fuels. Lithium-ion batteries are considered as the solution to above-mentioned concerns. However, high-cost of raw materials and lithium scarcity appear to be the challenges for this system in large-scale applications. Sodium is one of the most abundant elements in the earth crust and relatively high standard reduction potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode). Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are attracting considerable attention as a possible replacement for lithium-ion batteries due to the low-cost and availability of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0001_R_d82212598e347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0004_R_d82212598e347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Sustainable Syst.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Also, the existing production facilities of the lithium-ion batteries can be utilized for SIB production without any significant changes.</p>
         
         <p>In SIBs, the development of low-cost, highly efficient sodium-ion cathode materials is one of the most essential factors to meet the challenges of commercialization and large-scale applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0004_R_d82212598e355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Sustainable Syst.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Different categories of sodium-ion cathode materials are widely reported, such as sodium-based layered transition metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0005_R_d82212598e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Inorg. Chem.">5</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0007_R_d82212598e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Sci. Eng.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0008_R_d82212598e365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Electrochem. Commun.">8</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0011_R_d82212598e365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Sustainable Syst.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0012_R_d82212598e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Commun.">12</a>, <a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0013_R_d82212598e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sodium vanadium (fluoro-)phosphates,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0014_R_d82212598e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Electrochim. Acta">14</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0017_R_d82212598e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Sustainable Syst.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. Among these cathode material candidates mentioned above for the SIBs, the layered transition metal oxides (TMO<sub>2</sub>) attracted wide attention due to their high capacity, low cost, and relatively easier synthesis procedure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0018_R_d82212598e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0021_R_d82212598e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the layered transition metal oxides are classified into P-type oxide and O-type oxide, according to Delmas's convention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0022_R_d82212598e389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Phys. B+C">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> O3-type and P2-type sodium layered transition metal oxide are extensively studied for SIBs applications, where O and P represent the octahedral and prismatic environment, respectively, of the sodium ions, and 2 and 3 represent the number of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers in a unit cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0019_R_d82212598e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small">19</a>, <a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0020_R_d82212598e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chemistry">20</a>, <a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0023_R_d82212598e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is well-known that the P2-type oxides for Na<sup>+</sup> have a lower migration barrier due to a more open prismatic structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0020_R_d82212598e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chemistry">20</a>, <a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0024_R_d82212598e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0027_R_d82212598e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Also, P2-type oxides exhibit higher specific capacity and higher ionic conductivity despite having a few drawbacks such as irreversible phase transitions and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0020_R_d82212598e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chemistry">20</a>, <a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0028_R_d82212598e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Sci. Adv.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In P2-type oxides, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is widely studied because of having relatively high voltage derived from Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox couples and also Jahn–Teller distortion-free crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0029_R_d82212598e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Electrochim. Acta">29</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0031_R_d82212598e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the P2–O2 transition observed in the high-voltage plateau at 4.2&nbsp;V results in a large volume change leading to poor cyclability of the cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0032_R_d82212598e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to improve the structural stability and cycle life, the heteroatom substitution is considered a viable method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0031_R_d82212598e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Various atoms are substituted in the P2-type layered transition metal oxide such as Cu, Mg, Ti, Zn, Li, Ca, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0028_R_d82212598e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Sci. Adv.">28</a>, <a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0032_R_d82212598e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a>-<a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0043_R_d82212598e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these, Mg substitution is considered more promising for improvement in electrochemical properties of the cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0031_R_d82212598e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Magnesium being a light metal and electrochemically inactive, does not affect its energy density and specific capacity value much in the material level. The enhancement in the properties of the cathode materials by the Mg substitution is due to the structural stability and annihilation of the detrimental P2–OP4 phase transition. Tapia-Ruiz et&nbsp;al. reported Mg-substituted Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mg<i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> which effectively suppressed P2–O2 transition at high voltages and also disturbed Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering in the cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0033_R_d82212598e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wang et&nbsp;al. studied the P2-type Na<sub>0.7</sub>[Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> with Mg substitution in the Na sites, which acted as a “pillar” and stabilized the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0034_R_d82212598e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhang et&nbsp;al. reported the synthesis of P2-type Na<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by sol–gel method and studied the effect of Mg substitution on the suppression of detrimental P3-phase with enhanced electrochemical performances.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0035_R_d82212598e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The improvement in the cycle life and stability is attained by either due to suppression of the P3 phase or by pillaring effect of Mg that effectively avoids the sliding of the layers. However, the combination of these can significantly contribute to the NMO stability and its cycle life.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, Mg-substituted P2 type-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material is synthesized at different Mg substitution contents by using a simple solid-state route, and the effect of Mg substitution on the electrochemical performances such as capacity, cyclic behavior, and rate capability is evaluated. The synthesized Mg substituted layered transition metal oxide is extensively characterized by x-ray diffraction (XRD), scanning electron microscopy (SEM), energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS), and x-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). In this study, we observed the combined advantages of Mg-substitution, which resulted in the removal of the spurious P3-phase as well as substitution into the TM layer and Na-ion sites. This results in the enhancement of its electrochemical properties that have been established during the galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling, cycling stability, and rate studies. The optimized Mg substitution of 10% exhibits excellent mid-voltage of 3.5&nbsp;V and stability at different C-rates.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202200519-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Mg-substituted P2-type Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> is synthesized by typical solid-state synthesis by mixing corresponding oxides in high-energy planetary ball-milling followed by calcination in air atmosphere.Inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy(ICP-AES) analysis confirms the composition of the solid-state synthesized Mg-substituted P2-type is Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05, 0.1, and 0.15) as shown in <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adsu202200519-tbl-0001">1</a>. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the XRD patterns of the solid-state synthesized Mg-substituted P2-type Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> materials. All samples exhibit typical P2-type hexagonal crystal lattice structure in the space group of <i>P63/mmc</i>. It is noted that the trace amount of NiO impurity present in the samples. Apart from this, two interesting observations can be deduced from the powder XRD patterns. First, the diffraction peak that corresponds to the (002) crystal plane, shifts toward lower 2<i>θ</i>&nbsp;value with an increase in the Mg content up to a certain point and then slightly increases again. For 0% Mg, the 2<i>θ</i> is 15.96° which decreased to 15.89° when the Mg content increased to 10%. Upon further increase, the peak shifts back to 15.94° for the 15% Mg substitution. A similar observation was reported by Wang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0034_R_d82212598e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This analysis shows that the unit cell expands in the <i>c</i>-axis during Mg substitution up to 10% (from 11.144 to 11.163 Å) due to higher ionic radii of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (0.72 Å) versus Ni<sup>2+</sup> (0.69 Å). Upon further increase to 15% Mg substitution, there is a contraction in the <i>c</i>-axis (11.158 Å). The Rietveld refinement of the XRD patterns is carried out in all samples and the results are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a–d</a>, Supporting Information, and the detailed refinement and unit cell parameters are given in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S6</a>, Supporting Information. The Rietveld analysis shows that the Mg substitution takes place in both the transition-metal layer and sodium layer (above 10% Mg substitution) and it prefers to present in the transition metal layer at lower Mg substitution. When Mg<sup>2+</sup> ions enter the sodium layers, the contraction of the <i>c</i>-axis takes place (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>,<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). It is due to the smaller ionic radii of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and more positive charge than Na<sup>+</sup> (ionic radius:1.02 Å) that causes electrostatic attraction to the oxygen layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0034_R_d82212598e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, in addition to the P2-type hexagonal phase, P3-type crystal phase with the <i>R3̅m</i> space group can also be observed in the compound at the lower Mg substitution of 0% and 5%, which disappears in the 10% and 15% Mg substitution. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0001">1b</a> shows an enlarged version of the XRD patterns in the 2<i>θ</i> ranges from 30° to 50°. The P3 phase is quantitatively analyzed from the Rietveld refinement analysis and the result is presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information. The P3 phase reduces from 38.1% in NMO-0Mg to 1.6% in NMO-10Mg. However, there is a marginal increase in P3 phase of NMO-15Mg (5.8%). It is reported that the Mg substitution results in the P2 phase stabilization at high-temperature reactions due to the Mg substitution induced stronger Ni<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0034_R_d82212598e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The poor electrochemical stability and rate capability of P3 and P2/P3 hybrid layered sodium transition-metal oxide materials are reported in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0035_R_d82212598e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this case, the Mg substitution in the P2-type Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> suppresses the detrimental P3 phase formation.</p>

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202200519-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1ca7b1df-2b3b-4131-83e7-6e184afe1635/adsu202200519-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1ca7b1df-2b3b-4131-83e7-6e184afe1635/adsu202200519-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ffcfdb52-1296-4601-9e0a-fc0fa44aa3c2/adsu202200519-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1ca7b1df-2b3b-4131-83e7-6e184afe1635/adsu202200519-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202200519-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202200519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of solid-state synthesized P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05, 0.10, and 0.15). b) An enlarged version of the XRD patterns in (a) in the 2θ ranges from 30° to 50°.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphological studies of the cathode materials are carried out using field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM). <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0002">2</a> shows the FE-SEM images of the solid-state synthesized P2-type NMO-0Mg, NMO-5Mg, NMO-10Mg, and NMO-15Mg at higher magnification (50&nbsp;000×). The NMO-10Mg morphology shows typical irregular, sheet-like particles with uniform particle size distribution with particle size in the range of 5–50&nbsp;µm with the average particle size of 12.3&nbsp;µm. The BET surface area and particle size distribution are also carried out using surface area analyzer and particle size analyzer, respectively, and the values are close to what we observed in the SEM (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–d</a>, Supporting Information). It is to be noted that there is no larger variation in particle morphology is observed due to the addition of Mg in NMO. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0002">2e</a> shows the SEM-EDS X-ray elemental mapping of the NMO-10Mg sample. The EDS X-ray mapping of the NMO-10Mg displays the uniform distribution of metal ions such as Na, Ni, Mn, and Mg in the particles. There is no segregation or localized distribution of ions observed in the samples.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202200519-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bdb3c27f-be3a-4c06-b1e0-d1f24985150a/adsu202200519-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bdb3c27f-be3a-4c06-b1e0-d1f24985150a/adsu202200519-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eb035f8c-1a01-4fb6-a7b0-c6b9e5b6bfbe/adsu202200519-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bdb3c27f-be3a-4c06-b1e0-d1f24985150a/adsu202200519-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202200519-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202200519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the a–d) NMO-0Mg to NMO-15Mg at the magnification of 50&nbsp;000× and e) elemental X-ray mapping of the NMO-10Mg using SEM-EDS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is used to characterize the valence state and chemical environment of the ions in the synthesized materials. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the XPS survey scan spectra of the NMO-0Mg to NMO-15Mg. The spectra show the presence of identified elements such as Na, Ni, Mn, and Mg. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the high-resolution scan of elemental Mg2s for NMO-5Mg to NMO-15Mg. The high-resolution scans of the other individual elements such as Ni and Mn are carried out and the profile of the scans of each element is provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information. The Ni2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks are observed at 854.8&nbsp;eV for NMO-0Mg to NMO-10Mg and their corresponding satellite peaks at 861.8&nbsp;eV, which confirms the Ni<sup>2+</sup> in the composition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0035_R_d82212598e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the NMO-0Mg to NMO-10Mg samples show peaks at 642.04 and 653.0&nbsp;eV, which correspond to Mn2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, which show the Mn<sup>4+</sup> oxidation state. In the case of NMO-15Mg, the Ni2p and Mn2p peaks are shifted and increased by ≈2&nbsp;eV. The NMO-5Mg and NMO-10Mg show the characteristic Mg2s peak at 49.2&nbsp;eV, which corresponds to Mg<sup>2+,</sup> and a small peak of 52.0&nbsp;eV, which corresponds to MgO that may have been formed on exposure to the air atmosphere.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0035_R_d82212598e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the NMO-15Mg shows a significant increase in the peak at 52.0&nbsp;eV showing the different chemical environments of Mg in the lattice structure. That is, the Mg ions are substituted into the sodium layer apart from the transition metal layers which can be corroborated with our previous observation on XRD analysis.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202200519-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/766bcf2b-7646-4990-8081-b436ae0bd5b1/adsu202200519-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/766bcf2b-7646-4990-8081-b436ae0bd5b1/adsu202200519-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/facd6ff3-9745-4839-9215-5e473632f512/adsu202200519-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/766bcf2b-7646-4990-8081-b436ae0bd5b1/adsu202200519-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202200519-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202200519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Survey scan of X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy of NMO-0Mg to NMO-15Mg samples. b) High-resolution plots of Mg2s.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical characterization of the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode is investigated using galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD)&nbsp;studies. P2-type NaNiMnO<sub>2</sub> cathode materials are known for their severe capacity fading due to phase transition above 4.1&nbsp;V. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0004">4a–d</a> shows the initial five cycles of GCD behaviors of the NMO-0Mg, NMO-5Mg, NMO-10Mg, and NMO-15Mg in the voltage window of 2–4.3&nbsp;V at a C/10 rate. When compared to the NMO-0Mg, all samples NMO-5Mg, NMO-10Mg, and NMO-15Mg exhibit lower capacity due to the replacement of the electrochemically active Ni<sup>2+</sup> with the inactive Mg<sup>2+</sup>. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information, shows the effect of Mg-substitution in P2-type NMO on specific capacity and mid-voltage. The NMO-0Mg shows the 1st cycle initial specific discharge capacity 162.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> which upon further cycling reduces to 145 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the 5th cycle. Also, this NMO-0Mg exhibits several voltage plateaus during charge and discharge corresponding to the irreversible phase transitions. In the case of NMO-5Mg, those plateaus are still present with a specific capacity of 138 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> which falls to 135 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at cycle 5. Both NMO-0Mg and NMO-5Mg samples show &gt;10% of P3-phase that exhibits rapid deterioration of capacity, even though which exhibits high initial capacity. For NMO-10Mg and NMO-15Mg, the specific capacities are 104 and 85 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with better cycling stability. The reduction in specific capacity trends from NMO 0Mg to 15Mg can be easily correlated to the reduction in the stoichiometry of the Ni. Here Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox couple plays the main role in the electrochemical phenomenon. Thus, partially replacing Ni<sup>2+</sup> with Mg<sup>2+</sup> decreases the specific capacity. Also, NMO-0Mg and 5Mg show the 1st cycle coulombic efficiency of ≈70% whereas NMO-10Mg and NMO-15Mg exhibited &gt;80%. NMO-0Mg and NMO-5Mg show the initial capacity fading due to the irreversible transformation of the voltage plateau at ≈4.1&nbsp;V resulting from transition-metal layer gliding.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0028_R_d82212598e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Sci. Adv.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the case of NMO-10Mg and NMO-15Mg, the capacity fading is not observed to this extent. This shows that the Mg-substitution enhances the stability of the high-voltage Ni<sup>4+/3+/2+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0037_R_d82212598e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Sci.">37</a>, <a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0042_R_d82212598e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is also obvious from the charge/discharge curves that the curves are becoming smoother with an increase in the Mg substitution.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202200519-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d796dc64-634b-4ec9-ba5c-d69f4fa5f9da/adsu202200519-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d796dc64-634b-4ec9-ba5c-d69f4fa5f9da/adsu202200519-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/56106f56-1a3d-4e31-92c0-8941365fb7ce/adsu202200519-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d796dc64-634b-4ec9-ba5c-d69f4fa5f9da/adsu202200519-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202200519-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202200519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of 5 cycles a) NMO-0Mg, b) NMO-5Mg, c) NMO-10Mg, and d) NMO-15Mg measured in the voltage range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at C-rate of C/10.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0005">5</a> shows the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot of NMO-0Mg to NMO-15Mg obtained from the GCD studies, which shows the drastically changing charge/discharge behavior due to the incorporation of the Mg in the Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibits several redox peaks without Mg substitution due to the complex phase transformation and Na-ion vacancy ordering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0028_R_d82212598e1052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Sci. Adv.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are 11 sets of redox couples located between 2.4 to 4.2&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Among them, redox peaks located in the potential range of 3.0–3.75&nbsp;V can be attributed to the redox reactions of Ni<sup>2+</sup>–Ni<sup>3+</sup>–Ni<sup>3.5+</sup><sub>.</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0039_R_d82212598e1066" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks below 3&nbsp;V result due to redox reactions of the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> couple.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0042_R_d82212598e1075" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The number of redox couples present in the synthesized samples reduces with an increase in the concentration of Mg substitution. The reduction in the redox couples during the Mg substitution is clearly attributed to the suppression in the number of phase transformations and Na-ion vacancy ordering. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0006">6</a> shows Nyquist plot of the NMO-0Mg and NMO-10Mg along with the corresponding equivalent circuit used for fitting. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0006">6</a> inset shows the equivalent circuit used for fitting the experimental curve that simulates the electrode processes. <i>R</i><sub>sol</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>EEI</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>, and <i>R</i><sub>Elec</sub> correspond to the ohmic resistance of solution, electrode–electrolyte interface, charge-transfer, and bulk electronic resistance whereas <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> corresponds to the Warburg impedance associated with sodium diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0044_R_d82212598e1106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Commun. Chem.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is clear from the Nyquist plot that the NMO-0Mg has higher charge-transfer resistance when compared to the NMO-10Mg. The lower charge transfer resistance is mainly due to the Mg substitution that resulted in the high charge transfer capability and low interfacial barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202200519-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202200519-bib-0044_R_d82212598e1111" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Commun. Chem.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202200519-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a462d1df-b5df-465b-8bf0-63cbefc3d2db/adsu202200519-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a462d1df-b5df-465b-8bf0-63cbefc3d2db/adsu202200519-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b809e306-fd2c-4447-860d-5ce5bf024950/adsu202200519-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a462d1df-b5df-465b-8bf0-63cbefc3d2db/adsu202200519-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202200519-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202200519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–d) dQ/d<i>V</i> plots of NMO-0Mg to NMO-15Mg in the voltage range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V at the C-rate of C/10.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202200519-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/458a25d5-3281-4a44-9a03-663a12c1806e/adsu202200519-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/458a25d5-3281-4a44-9a03-663a12c1806e/adsu202200519-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2858a5c1-75e2-4994-87e3-bafb413e534a/adsu202200519-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/458a25d5-3281-4a44-9a03-663a12c1806e/adsu202200519-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202200519-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202200519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Nyquist plot of NMO-0Mg and NMO-10Mg measured at the potential of 3.5&nbsp;V (Inset 1: Equivalent circuit diagram used for fitting the impedance).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The rate capability and cycling stability studies of the synthesized NMO materials are investigated at different C-rates and cycling up to 100 cycles. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0007">7a</a> shows the NMO-10Mg charge–discharge plots of the first four cycles. As we can see, there is a negligible reduction in the capacity of the cathode materials with cycle. Also, the material exhibits excellent coulombic efficiency &gt;99%. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0007">7b</a> shows the rate capability and cycle stability up to 100 cycles of NMO-0Mg and NMO-10Mg at the C rates of C/10, C/5, C/2, 1C and 2C. The NMO-10Mg displays excellent cycling stability and high coulombic efficiency up to 100 cycles at the C/5 rate as provided. The NMO-10Mg shows the initial specific capacity of 104 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and is reduced marginally to 103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles. The cycle-to-cycle coulombic efficiency is maintained at more than 98%. The full-cell level pouch (single electrode −10 cm<sup>2</sup>) is demonstrated with solid-state synthesized P2-type Mg-substituted NMO versus hard carbon. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0007">7c</a> shows the galvanostatic charge-discharge cycle of the full-cell. The cell shows a capacity of ≈7.6 mAh and capacity utilization is 90 mAh g<sub>cathode</sub><sup>−1</sup>. The full-cell CV carried out at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage window of 2–4.3&nbsp;V is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0007">7c</a> inset. As we can see that the CV is showing good reversibility of the cell. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0007">7d</a> shows the cyclic stability and efficiency of full-cell pouch cell carried out at C/5 rate up to 60 cycles. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202200519-fig-0007">7d</a> inset shows the demonstration of working model of a sodium-ion cell by using red LED.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202200519-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e3ba7b6-7273-427e-ae8c-9eeee6f20e3e/adsu202200519-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e3ba7b6-7273-427e-ae8c-9eeee6f20e3e/adsu202200519-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a3e69a7c-4756-4313-b3c6-314a1e7d82fd/adsu202200519-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e3ba7b6-7273-427e-ae8c-9eeee6f20e3e/adsu202200519-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202200519-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202200519" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge cycles of NMO-10Mg (Cycle-1, 5,10, and 40). b) rate capability and cycle stability studies of NMO-0Mg and NMO-10Mg at different C-rates in the range of C/10 to 2C. c) cycle of a full-cell pouch in the voltage range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V at C/10 rate (1&nbsp;mA). Inset of (c) cyclic voltammetry of NMO-10Mg||Hard carbon in the voltage range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) cyclic stability and efficiency of full-cell pouch cell carried out at C/5 rate up to 60 cycles. Inset of (d): demonstration of a working model of a sodium-ion cell by using red LED.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202200519-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have synthesized Mg substituted P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by a simple solid-state method with different Mg substitutions ranging from 0% to 15% in place of Ni. XRD analysis revealed that Mg substitution suppresses the detrimental P3 phase formation starting from 0% to 10%, however increases marginally with 15% Mg. Rietveld refinement confirms <i>c</i>-axis as well as volume expansion in 0–10% Mg substituted materials however it shows an opposite trend upon 15% Mg substitution. Also from the refinement data, it was seen that with 15% Mg substitution, Mg enters into the Na layers along with TM layer corroborating to the two different peaks in XPS data. Mg substitution at 10% showed excellent electrochemical performance in both half-cell level and full cell level, which can be considered for the practical Na-ion cells. Despite the reduction in specific capacity with an increase in Mg substitution, the mid-voltage reaches the maximum of 3.5&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at 10% of Mg. Further addition of Mg to 15% reduces the mid-voltage substantially to 3.25&nbsp;V accompanied by the specific capacity loss. 10% Mg substituted P2-NMO shows excellent rate capability up to 2C rate and cycling stability up to 100 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202200519-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202200519-sec-0070-title"> Synthesis of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub></h4>
                  
                  <p>All chemicals, namely, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (99%), NiO (99%), MnO<sub>2</sub> (99%), and MgO (99%) were procured from Alfa Aesar, India, and used without further purification. A typical solid-state route was adopted for synthesizing Na<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.45-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> samples with magnesium substitution in the range of 0–15% using metal oxides and sodium carbonate as precursors. Briefly, the stoichiometric ratios of metal oxides and sodium carbonate were taken in an yttria-stabilized zirconia bowl along with zirconia balls. 5&nbsp;mol% extra sodium carbonate was taken considering the loss of sodium during the heating process. Isopropanol was used as the solvent to ensure uniform mixing. The mixture was intimately mixed using a planetary ball mill (Fritsch Pulverisette 7, Germany) for 20&nbsp;h at 350&nbsp;rpm. After mixing, the slurry was thoroughly dried by heating overnight at 80&nbsp;°C under a vacuum. The solid mass was transferred to an alumina crucible and preheated up to 500&nbsp;°C for 5&nbsp;h followed by 850&nbsp;°C for 10&nbsp;h. Then the samples were allowed to cool naturally to room temperature to get the final products. After the reaction, the samples were immediately transferred to an argon-filled glovebox to avoid moisture exposure till further use. The samples were identified as NMO-0Mg, NMO-5Mg, NMO-10Mg, and NMO-15Mg corresponding to Mg content of 0%, 5%, 10%, and 15%, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202200519-sec-0080-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The structural characteristic of the as-prepared materials was determined by X-ray diffraction (XRD, D8 Discover, Bruker) with filtered Cu K<sub><i>α</i></sub> radiation in the 2<i>θ</i> range of 10° to 80° with the step size of 0.02°. Rietveld refinement analysis was carried out using HighScore Plus software. The morphology of the samples was observed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (Gemini SEM 500, FE-SEM) and the elemental distribution was obtained by X-ray mapping using energy dispersive spectroscopy (Bruker QuantaxXFlash, EDS). The chemical compositions of the sample materials were examined by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy analysis (ICP-AES, OPTIMA 4300&nbsp;V, PerkinElmer), while sodium estimation was done by flame photometry. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy was carried out by the instrument (XPS, PHI 5000 VersaProbe II, ULVAC-PHI Inc., USA) equipped with a micro-focused (200&nbsp;µm, 15&nbsp;kV) monochromatic Al-K<sub><i>α</i></sub> X-Ray source (<i>hν</i> = 1486.6&nbsp;eV). Both survey spectra and narrow scans (high-resolution spectra) were recorded. Survey scans were recorded with an X-ray source power of 50&nbsp;W and pass energy of 187.85&nbsp;eV. High-resolution spectra of the major elements were recorded at 46.95&nbsp;eV pass energy. XPS data were processed using XPSPeak fit the software. The binding energy was referenced to the C1s peak at 284.8&nbsp;eV. The surface area of the cathode materials was analyzed by using Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area analyzer (Quantachrome Nova 1200e, USA). The average particle size and particle size distribution were obtained by laser diffraction particle size analyzer (Malvern Master sizer 2000, UK).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202200519-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical performances of the cathode materials were evaluated against metallic sodium (Na, 99.95%, Sigma-Aldrich) half-cell in CR2032 coin cell configuration. All the cell assembly processes were carried out in an argon-filled glove box with H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> content &lt; 0.1&nbsp;ppm to avoid the exposure to moisture. The positive electrodes were processed by mixing synthesized cathode materials, acetylene black (AB), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in a weight ratio of 80:10:10 as the active material, conducting carbon and binder, respectively, in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone solvent. Then the homogenous slurries were coated on the aluminum foil (20&nbsp;µm thickness) using a doctor blade. The active material loading of 9.0&nbsp;±&nbsp;0.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> was obtained. After coating, the wet electrodes were dried in a vacuum oven at 80&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h and compacted at 80&nbsp;°C in a hot roll press (MTI, USA). The compacted electrodes were punched into the circular disk of 15&nbsp;mm diameter. The coin cells were assembled with a positive electrode against metallic Na foil as the anode. 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC), diethyl carbonate (DEC), and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) (47.5:47.5:5, v/v) was used as an electrolyte. For the cyclic voltammetry studies and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy measurement, a biologic test system (VMP3, France) was used. For CV studies, the voltage window was 2.0–4.3&nbsp;V with a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling was done in the voltage range of 2.0–4.3&nbsp;V on a battery testing system (Neware). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was carried out at the OCV in the frequency range of 1&nbsp;MHz to 10 mHz with the voltage amplitude of 5&nbsp;mV. The full-cell level studies were carried out by using hard carbon as the anode and NMO-10Mg as the cathode in a 10 cm<sup>2</sup> area single electrode pouch cell. For the anode, the slurry was prepared by mixing hard carbon, sodium carboxymethyl cellulose, and styrene-butadiene rubber with a weight ratio of 90:5:5 in deionized water and was coated over the copper foil. The electrodes were dried at 120&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h and compacted using a hot roll press at 80&nbsp;°C. The cathode/anode mass ratio was kept at 2.5&nbsp;±&nbsp;0.1. The galvanostatic charge–discharge was carried out at the rate of C/10 in the voltage range of 2.0–4.3&nbsp;V. The rate studies were carried out at C/5, C/2, 1C, and 2C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202200519-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202200519-sec-0100-title"> Associated Content</h4>
                  
                  <p>The following files are available free of charge. XRD Rietveld refinement plots and tables; XPS high resolution scans of Ni2p and Mn2p; Specific capacity and mid-voltage graph; Particle size distribution graphs; Table of surface area and particle size.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
